the direct unitary implementation. In fact, for short control–target separations, the unitary circuit achieved higher fidelity. However, at larger separations, the dynamic circuit begins to achieve better fidelity than the unitary implementation. This behavior is not unexpected on current hardware: while dynamic circuits reduce circuit depth by avoiding long SWAP chains, they introduce additional circuit time from mid-circuit measurements, classical feedforward, and control-path delays. The added latency increases decoherence and readout errors, which can outweigh the depth savings at short distances.","className":"group paragraph-el text-text-primary mx-0"}],"\n",["$","p",null,{"children":"Nevertheless, we observe a crossover point where the dynamic approach surpasses the unitary one. constant.","className":"group paragraph-el text-text-primary mx-0"}],"\n",["$","p",null,{"children":["$","strong",null,{"children":"Key points:"}],"className":"group paragraph-el text-text-primary implemented with dynamic circuits: performance remains essentially constant as the separation between control and target qubits increases. These measurements are efficient, since they are performed in parallel, but they still introduce a fixed cost on today’s hardware.","className":"group paragraph-el text-text-primary mx-0"}],"\n",["$","p",null,{"children":"Why fidelity can be worse today: Increased circuit time from measurement and classical operations often dominates, especially when the control-target separation is small.
